Since the slope of the line is $\frac{2}{5},$ the line rises 2 units vertically for every 5 horizontal units.  Thus, a possible direction vector is $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = (0,0);
B = (5,0);
C = (5,2);

draw(A--B--C);
draw(A--C,red,Arrow(6));

label("$5$", (A + B)/2, S);
label("$2$", (B + C)/2, E);
[/asy]

This means any nonzero scalar multiple of $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}$ is a possible direction vector.  The possible options are then $\boxed{\text{B, E, G}}.$

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/precalculus/101.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Setting the parameterizations to be equal, we obtain
\begin{align*}
1 + 2t + 2s &= 7 + 3u, \\
6 - t - 3s &= 4 \\
7 - t - 5s &= 1 - u.

The line containing $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ can be parameterized by
\[\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{a} + t (\mathbf{b} - \mathbf{a}) = \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]Since $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ must be equal to the angle between $\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{c}.$  Thus,
\[\frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\|\mathbf{b}\| \|\mathbf{c}\|}.\]Then $\frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\|\mathbf{c}\|},$ so
\[\frac{\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix} \right\|}.\]Hence,

Let the circumcenter $O$ of triangle $ABC$ be the origin.  Then
\[\overrightarrow{G} = \frac{\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}}{3}\]and $\overrightarrow{H} = \overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C},$ so
\[\overrightarrow{F} = \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}).\]Then
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= \|\overrightarrow{A} - \overrightarrow{F}\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}) \right\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \frac{1}{3} \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{B} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{C} \right\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \|\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \cdot (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{A} + 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 4 \overrightarrow{C} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}  - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}), \\
CF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Thus, $AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 = \boxed{3R^2}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Note that
\[3AF^2 = AG^2 + BG^2 + CG^2 + GH^2 - AH^2 - BH^2 - CH^2.\]First, we have that $AG^2 + BG^2 + CG^2 = 3(GA^2 + GB^2 + GC^2) = 3\left( \frac13 a^2 + \frac13 b^2 + \frac13 c^2 \right) = a^2 + b^2 + c^2.$

Since $G$ is the centroid, we have that $AG = \frac23 AD,$ $BG = \frac23 BE,$ and $CG = \frac23 CF.$  Hence,
\[AG^2 = \frac49 AD^2 = \frac29 (b^2 + c^2) - \frac19 a^2,\]and similarly,
\[BG^2 = \frac29 (a^2 + c^2) - \frac19 b^2 \quad \text{and} \quad CG^2 = \frac29 (a^2 + b^2) - \frac19 c^2.\]Therefore,
\[AG^2 + BG^2 + CG^2 = \frac49 (a^2 + b^2 + c^2) - \frac19 (a^2 + b^2 + c^2) = \frac13 (a^2 + b^2 + c^2).\]Next, we have $GH = 2OG = 2R.$  Also, $AH = 2R \cos A,$ etc.  Therefore,
\[AH^2 = 4R^2 \cos^2 A = 4R^2 - a^2.\]Similarly, $BH^2 = 4R^2 - b^2$ and $CH^2 = 4R^2 - c^2.$

Putting everything together, we get
\[\begin{aligned} 3AF^2 &= a^2 + b^2 + c^2 + (2R)^2 - (4R^2 - a^2) - (4R^2 - b^2) - (4R^2 - c^2) \\
&= a^2 + b^2 + c^2 + 12R^2 - 8R^2 + a^2 + b^2 + c^2 = 2(a^2 + b^2 + c^2) + 4R^2. \end{aligned}\]Hence, $AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 = \boxed{2(a^2 + b^2 + c^2) + 12R^2}.$

Setting $t = 0,$ we get
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = \mathbf{v}.\]But the distance between $\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix}$ and $\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix}$ is $t = 0,$ so $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix}.$  Thus,
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix} + t \mathbf{d}.\]Then for $x \le 7,$
\[\left\| \begin{pmatrix} x - 7 \\ y + 2 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \left\| \begin{pmatrix} x - 7 \\ \frac{-12x + 84}{5} \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \left\| \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -\frac{12}{5} \end{pmatrix} \right\| (7 - x) = \frac{13}{5} (7 - x).\]We want this to be $t,$ so $t = \frac{13}{5} (7 - x).$  Then $x = 7 - \frac{5}{13} t,$ and $y = \frac{-12x + 74}{5} = \frac{12}{13} t - 2,$ so

From the formula for a projection,
\[\operatorname{proj}_{\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\|^2} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]This vector has magnitude
\[\left\| \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \cdot 2 = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2}.\]Thus, we want $\frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2} = \sqrt{3}.$  Equivalently, $|a \sqrt{3} + b| = 2 \sqrt{3},$ or $(a \sqrt{3} + b)^2 = 12.$

Also, $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ so
\[(2 \sqrt{3} + 4b)^2 = 12.\]Then $2 \sqrt{3} + 4b = \pm 2 \sqrt{3}.$  This leads to the solutions $b = -\sqrt{3}$ and $b = 0,$ which in turn leads to the values $a = \boxed{-1}$ and $a = \boxed{2}.$

The projection of $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix}$ onto $\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ is
\[\frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.\][asy]
usepackage("amsmath");

unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, P;

P = (2*sqrt(3),4);

A = (0,0);
B = (4*sqrt(3),0);

draw((-0.5,0)--(4.5,0));
draw((0,-0.5)--(0,4.5));
draw(A--P--(P + (B - P)/sqrt((B - P).(B - P)))--cycle);
draw(P--(P + (B - P)/sqrt((B - P).(B - P))),dashed);

label("$\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix}$", A, SW);
label("$\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$", B, E);
label("$\frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$", (P + (B - P)/sqrt((B - P).(B - P)))/2, NE);
[/asy]

Hence,
\[\left\| \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{3}.\]Squaring both sides, we get
\[\frac{(a \sqrt{3} + b)^2}{16} = 3.\]Then
\[a^2 + 2ab \sqrt{3} + 3b^2 = 16 \cdot 3 = 48.\]Also, $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ so
\[(2 + b \sqrt{3})^2 + 2b (2 + b \sqrt{3}) \sqrt{3} + 3b^2 = 48.\]Then $a^2 = 19b^2 + 8$ and $a = -2 - b \sqrt{3}.$  We get
\[(-2 - b \sqrt{3})^2 + 2b (-2 - b \sqrt{3}) \sqrt{3} + 3b^2 = 48.\]Hence, $a^2 = 19b^2 + 8$ is valid for $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3}$ and $a = -2 - b \sqrt{3}.$

For $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ $a^2 = (2 + b \sqrt{3})^2 = 19b^2 + 8,$ so $19b^2 = (a^2 - 8).$  Then $a^2 - 8$ must be a multiple of 19.  Checking the residues of perfect squares mod 19, we find that $a^2 \equiv 8,$ 11, 12, or 16 mod 19.  Hence, we must have $a^2 \equiv 8 \pmod{19}.$

Since 19 is not of the form $4k + 3,$ $-3$ is a square mod 19, say $(x,y) = (1,5).$  Then
\[8 = 5^2 - 3 = (5 - 1)(5 + 1).\]Thus, the possible residues of $a^2$ modulo 19 are 2 and 17.  We quickly check that these correspond to the possible residues of $a$ modulo 19, namely $\pm 3,$ $\pm 5,$ $\pm 7,$ $\pm 11,$ or $\pm 12.$

Therefore, the possible values of $a$ are $\boxed{\sqrt{57} + 3, -\sqrt{57} - 3, -\sqrt{57} + 5, \sqrt{57} - 5, -3 - \sqrt{57}, 3 + \sqrt{57}, -7 - \sqrt{57}, 7 + \sqrt{57}}.$

The function $f(x)$ is defined for $-1 \le x \le 1.$

Note that as $x$ goes from $-1$ to 1, $\arctan x$ goes from $-\frac{\pi}{4}$ to $\frac{\pi}{4},$ and $\arcsin x$ goes from $-\frac{\pi}{2}$ to $\frac{\pi}{2}.$  Hence, $f(x)$ goes from $-\frac{\pi}{4} - \frac{\pi}{4} = -\frac{\pi}{2}$ to $\frac{\pi}{4} + \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{3 \pi}{4}.$

Hence, the range of $f(x)$ is $\boxed{\left[ -\frac{\pi}{2}, \frac{3 \pi}{4} \right]}.$

\[\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -y + z \\ x - z \\ -x + y \end{pmatrix}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
-y + z &= 1, \\
x - z &= -2, \\
-x + y &= 1.
\end{align*}Solving this system, along with the equation $x + y + z = 2,$ we find $x = -\frac{1}{3},$ $y = \frac{2}{3},$ and $z = \frac{5}{3}.$  Thus, $\mathbf{v} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -1/3 \\ 2/3 \\ 5/3 \end{pmatrix}}.$

\[\operatorname{proj}_{\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\][asy]

From product-to-sum,
\[\sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ = \frac{1}{2} [\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ].\]Thus, we can make the sum telescope:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ &= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \frac{\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2 \sin 5^\circ} [(\cos 0^\circ - \cos 10^\circ) + (\cos 5^\circ - \cos 15^\circ) + (\cos 10^\circ - \cos 20^\circ) + \\
&\quad + \dots + (\cos 165^\circ - \cos 175^\circ) + (\cos 170^\circ - \cos 180^\circ)] \\
&= \frac{\cos 0^\circ + \cos 5^\circ - \cos 175^\circ - \cos 180^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 + 2 \cos 5^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ}.
\end{align*}Then by the double-angle formulas,
\begin{align*}
\frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} &= \frac{1 + 2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ - 1}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 2.5^\circ}{\sin 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \cot 2.5^\circ \\
&= \tan 87.5^\circ.
\end{align*}Thus, $r = \boxed{87.5}.$

Let $S = \sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\sin 5^\circ S &= \sin 5^\circ \sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sum_{k=1}^{35} (\cos (5(k - 1))^\circ - \cos (5(k + 1))^\circ) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} (\cos 5^\circ - \cos 175^\circ + \cos 10^\circ - \cos 170^\circ + \dots + \cos 170^\circ - \cos 10^\circ + \cos 175^\circ - \cos 5^\circ) \\
&= 0,
\end{align*}where we use multiple angles (e.g. $\cos 10^\circ = \cos (5^\circ + 5^\circ)$).

Furthermore,
\begin{align*}
\cos 5^\circ S &= \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin (5k - 90)^\circ \\
&= \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \cos (5(k - 1) - 90)^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{2} (\sin 85^\circ + \sin 80^\circ + \dots + \sin (-80^\circ) + \sin (-85^\circ)) \\
&= \frac{\cos \frac{5}{2}^\circ - \cos \frac{175}{2}^\circ}{2 \sin \frac{5}{2}^\circ}.
\end{align*}Then
\begin{align*}
\tan r^\circ &= \frac{\sin 5^\circ S}{\cos 5^\circ S} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin \frac{5}{2}^\circ \cos \frac{175}{2}^\circ}{\cos \frac{5}{2}^\circ - \cos \frac{175}{2}^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin \frac{5}{2}^\circ \sin \frac{5}{2}^\circ}{\sin (90^\circ - 175/2)^\circ - \sin (90^\circ - 5/2)^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin^2 \frac{5}{2}^\circ}{\cos \frac{5}{2}^\circ + \cos \frac{175}{2}^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin^2 \frac{5}{2}^\circ}{2 \cos \frac{90}{2}^\circ \cos \frac{85}{2}^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin^2 \frac{5}{2}^\circ}{\sin 2^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin^2 \frac{5}{2}^\circ}{2 \sin 1^\circ \cos 1^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin^2 2.5^\circ}{2 \sin 1^\circ \cos 1^\circ},
\end{align*}so $r = \boxed{5}.$

Let $\mathbf{a}$ be an arbitrary vector.  Let $\mathbf{p}$ be the projection of $\mathbf{a}$ onto $\mathbf{v},$ so $\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a},$ and let $\mathbf{r}$ be the reflection of $\mathbf{a}$ over $\mathbf{v},$ to $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{R} \mathbf{a}.$

Note that $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r}.$  We can use this to find the relationship between $\mathbf{R}$ and $\mathbf{P}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair D, P, R, V;

D = (3,2);
V = (1.5,2);
R = reflect((0,0),D)*(V);
P = (V + R)/2;

draw((-1,0)--(4,0));
draw((0,-1)--(0,3));
draw((0,0)--D,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--V,red,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--R,blue,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--P,green,Arrow(6));
draw(V--R,dashed);

label("$\mathbf{v}$", D, NE);
label("$\mathbf{p}$", P, S);
label("$\mathbf{a}$", V, N);
label("$\mathbf{r}$", R, SE);
[/asy]

Since $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r},$ $\mathbf{p} = \frac{\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{r}}{2},$ so
\[\mathbf{r} = 2 \mathbf{p} - \mathbf{a}.\]In other words,
\[\mathbf{R} \mathbf{a} = 2 \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a} - \mathbf{I} \mathbf{a}.\]Since this holds for all vectors $\mathbf{a},$
\[\mathbf{R} = 2 \mathbf{P} - \mathbf{I}.\]Thus, $(a,b) = \boxed{(2,-1)}.$

\[\mathbf{D} \mathbf{R} = \begin{pmatrix} k & 0 \\ 0 & k \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} \cos \theta & -\sin \theta \\ \sin \theta & \cos \theta \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} k \cos \theta & -k \sin \theta \\ k \sin \theta & k \cos \theta \end{pmatrix}.\]Thus, $k \cos \theta = -7$ and $k \sin \theta = -1.$  Then
\[k^2 \cos^2 \theta + k^2 \sin^2 \theta = 49 + 1 = 50,\]which simplifies to $k^2 = 50.$  Since $k > 0,$ $k = \sqrt{50} = \boxed{5 \sqrt{2}}.$

Applying the triple angle formula, we get
\begin{align*}
\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x &= \left( \frac{3}{4} \sin 2x - \frac{1}{4} \sin 6x \right) \cos 6x + \left( \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x + \frac{1}{4} \cos 6x \right) \sin 6x \\
&= \frac{3}{4} \sin 2x \cos 6x + \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x \sin 6x.
\end{align*}Then by the angle addition formula,
\[\frac{3}{4} \sin 2x \cos 6x + \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x \sin 6x = \frac{3}{4} \sin (2x + 6x) = \frac{3}{4} \sin 8x.\]Thus, $a + b = \frac{3}{4} + 8 = \boxed{\frac{35}{4}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We can write
\begin{align*}
\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x &= \sin 2x \cos 2x (\sin^2 2x \cos 6x + \cos^2 2x \sin 6x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x (\sin^2 2x \cos 6x + \cos^2 2x \sin 6x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x [\sin^2 2x (1 - \sin^2 6x) + \cos^2 2x \sin 6x] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x [\sin^2 2x + \cos^2 2x \sin 6x - \sin^2 2x \sin^2 6x] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x [\sin^2 2x + \cos^2 2x \sin 6x - \sin^2 2x (1 - \cos^2 6x)] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x [\sin^2 2x + \cos^2 2x \sin 6x - \sin^2 2x + \sin^2 2x \cos^2 6x] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x [\cos^2 2x + \sin^2 2x \cos^2 6x + \cos^2 2x \sin 6x] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x [\cos^2 2x + \cos^2 2x \sin 6x + \sin^2 2x \cos^2 6x] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x [\cos^2 2x + (\cos^2 2x - \cos^2 8x) + (\cos^2 2x + \cos^2 8x)] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x (2 \cos^2 2x + \cos^2 8x) \\
&= \sin 2x \cos 2x (2 \cos^2 2x + \cos^2 8x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x (2 \cos^2 2x + \cos^2 8x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} (2 \cos^2 2x + 1 + 2 \cos^2 2x - 1)(1 - \cos^2 4x) \\
&= 2 \cos^2 2x \sin^2 2x (1 - \cos^2 4x) \\
&= 2 \cos^2 2x \sin^2 2x \sin^2 2x \\
&= 2 \cdot \frac{1}{2} (1 + \cos 4x) \cdot \frac{1 - \cos 4x}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{4} \cdot 2 (1 - \cos^2 4x) \\
&= \frac{1}{4} \sin^2 4x \\
&= \frac{1}{4} \sin^2 (2 \cdot 2x).
\end{align*}Thus, $a + b = \frac{1}{4} + 2 = \boxed{\frac{9}{4}}.$

We have that $\tan B = \frac{3}{2}$ and $\tan C = \frac{1}{2},$ so
\[\frac{\sin B}{\cos B} = \frac{3}{2} \quad\text{and}\quad \frac{\sin C}{\cos C} = \frac{1}{2}.\]Hence,
\[\frac{\sin B}{\sin C} = \frac{3}{1} \quad\text{and}\quad \frac{\cos B}{\cos C} = \frac{2}{1},\]so by the Law of Sines,
\[\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{c}{\sin C} = \frac{a + b + c}{2R},\]where $A + B + C = 180^\circ.$  Then $a = b \cdot \frac{\sin A}{\sin B}$ and $c = b \cdot \frac{\sin C}{\sin B},$ so
\[a = b \cdot \frac{\sin A}{\sin B} = b \cdot \frac{\sin 180^\circ}{\sin B - \sin C} = 2b.\]Hence, $a + b + c = 3b = 32,$ so $b = \frac{32}{3}.$  Then $a = \frac{64}{3}$ and $c = \frac{16}{3}.$

[asy]

label("$b$", (A + C)/2, NE);
label("$c$", (B + C)/2 + (0,-0.3));
[/asy]

\[b \cdot \frac{\cos A}{\sin B} = \frac{16}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{5} = \frac{64}{15},\]so the area of triangle $ABC$ is
\[\frac{1}{2} bc \sin A = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{32}{3} \cdot \frac{16}{3} \cdot \frac{15}{64} = \boxed{\frac{40}{3}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $AD = x$, and let $F$ be the point on line $DE$ such that $BF \parallel DE$ and $BF$ intersects $AC$ at $G$. Then we can use similar triangles to obtain that $AG = \frac{x^2}{20}$ while $GC = \frac{(10-x)^2}{20}$. We also have $FC = BF + BC + CG = BC + 2 \cdot \frac{(10-x)^2}{20}$, so to find $x$, we just need to solve the following equation:

\begin{align*}
\frac{x^2}{20} &= \frac{(10-x)^2}{20} + BC + \frac{(10-x)^2}{10} \\
&= \frac{(10-x)^2}{20} + 6 + \frac{(10-x)^2}{10}.
\end{align*}

\begin{align*}
\frac{e^{2i \theta} - 1}{e^{2i \theta} + 1} &= \frac{(e^{2i \theta} - 1)(e^{-2i \theta} - 1)}{(e^{2i \theta} + 1)(e^{-2i \theta} - 1)} \\

&= \boxed{-\sec \theta}.

\[2i \sin 2 \theta = e^{2i \theta} - e^{-2i \theta}.\]

From the angle addition formula, the numerator is
\begin{align*}
&(\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) + (\cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ -  \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos (5^\circ + 20^\circ) + \cos (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ.
\end{align*}From the sum-to-product formula, $\cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ = 2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ.$

Similarly, the denominator is
\begin{align*}
&\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= (\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ +  \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) - (\sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin (5^\circ + 20^\circ) - \sin (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin 25^\circ - \sin 85^\circ \\
&= -2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ,
\end{align*}so the expression is equal to
\[\frac{2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ}{-2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ} = -\frac{\cos 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ} = -\sqrt{3} = \tan 120^\circ.\]Hence, the smallest such $\theta$ is $\boxed{120^\circ}.$

We see that angle addition formulae frequently appear in trigonometry problems, so we attempt to make the somewhat awkward expression in the question into an expression in which we can make use of such formulae. Having the $\sin$ terms and the $\cos$ terms separated doesn't look too helpful in this regard, so we attempt to multiply the numerator and denominator by $\cos 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ$:
\[\begin{aligned} \frac{\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ} &= \frac{\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ \cos 20^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ \cos 20^\circ} \\ &= \frac{\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \cos 50^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 20^\circ \sin 50^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \cos 50^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \cos 20^\circ \sin 50^\circ} \\ &= \frac{(\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 20^\circ) \cos 50^\circ - (\sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ + \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \cos 20^\circ}{(\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) \cos 50^\circ - (\sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \cos 20^\circ} \\
&= \frac{(\cos 20^\circ + \cos 35^\circ) \cos 50^\circ - (\sin 5^\circ + \sin 35^\circ) \sin 20^\circ}{(\sin 5^\circ + \cos 5^\circ) \cos 20^\circ - (\sin 35^\circ + \cos 35^\circ) \sin 50^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 20^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ},
\end{aligned}\]where in the last step we have used the fact that $\cos(180^\circ-x) = -\cos x$ and $\sin(180^\circ - x) = \sin x$.

Now we can use the formula for $\tan(\alpha + \beta)$ to simplify the expression to: \[\tan(\alpha + \beta) = \frac{\tan \alpha + \tan \beta}{1 - \tan \alpha \tan \beta}.\]Taking $\alpha = 20^\circ$ and $\beta = 30^\circ$, we get: \[\tan 50^\circ = \frac{\tan 20^\circ + \tan 30^\circ}{1 - \tan 20^\circ \tan 30^\circ}.\]Note that the angle we seek - the least positive angle $\theta$ - is the angle that satisfies $\tan \theta = \tan 50^\circ$, or $\theta = 50^\circ + 180^\circ k$ for some integer $k$. So it suffices to solve for $\theta$ in $50^\circ = \arctan \left(\frac{\tan 20^\circ + \frac{1}{\sqrt 3}}{1 - \frac{\tan 20^\circ}{\sqrt 3}}\right)$, which is about \[50^\circ = 68.01664468^\circ.\]Thus the least such $\theta$ is $68.01664468^\circ - 50^\circ = \boxed{18.01664468^\circ}$.

We have that the vector $\overrightarrow{OA}$ is parallel to the vector $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix},$ so there exists a scalar $r$ such that $\overrightarrow{OA} = r \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}.$  Solving, we find $r = \sqrt{14}.$

Similarly, the vector $\overrightarrow{OB}$ is parallel to the vector $\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ -\frac{2}{3} \\ \frac{1}{3} \end{pmatrix},$ so there exists a scalar $s$ such that $\overrightarrow{OB} = s \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ -\frac{2}{3} \\ \frac{1}{3} \end{pmatrix}.$  Solving, we find $s = \sqrt{\frac{6}{7}}.$

Then the area of triangle $OAB$ is half the magnitude of the vector $\overrightarrow{OA} \times \overrightarrow{OB}.$

\begin{align*}
\overrightarrow{OA} \times \overrightarrow{OB} &= \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ -2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \begin{pmatrix} -8 \\ -8 \\ 8 \end{pmatrix}.
\end{align*}Hence, the area of triangle $OAB$ is $\frac{1}{2} \|\overrightarrow{OA} \times \overrightarrow{OB}\| = \boxed{4 \sqrt{2}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
First, note that $1998^\circ+237^\circ=2235^\circ$, which is a multiple of $360^\circ$, so $\sin(1998^\circ+237^\circ)=\sin(237^\circ)$. Similarly, $1998^\circ-1653^\circ=345^\circ$, which is not a multiple of $360^\circ$, but $\sin(345^\circ)=-\sin(15^\circ)$. Hence, we have  \begin{align*}
\sin(1998^\circ+237^\circ)\sin(1998^\circ-1653^\circ)&=\sin(237^\circ)\cdot (-\sin(15^\circ))\\
&=-\frac{1}{2}(\cos(222^\circ)-\cos(252^\circ))\cdot \frac{1}{2}(\cos(15^\circ)-\cos(285^\circ))\\
&=-\frac{1}{4}(\cos(222^\circ)-\cos(252^\circ))(\cos(15^\circ)+\cos(75^\circ)).
\end{align*}Using our knowledge of cosines of common angles, we have $\cos 15^\circ = \frac{\sqrt{6}+\sqrt{2}}{4}$ and $\cos 75^\circ = \frac{\sqrt{6}-\sqrt{2}}{4}$. It follows that  \begin{align*}
\sin(1998^\circ+237^\circ)\sin(1998^\circ-1653^\circ)&= -\frac{1}{4}\left(\cos 222^\circ -\cos 252^\circ\right)\left(\frac{\sqrt{6}+\sqrt{2}}{4} + \frac{\sqrt{6}-\sqrt{2}}{4}\right)\\
&= -\frac{1}{4}\left(\cos 222^\circ -\cos 252^\circ\right)\cdot \frac{\sqrt{6}}{2}\\
&= -\frac{1}{4}\left(-\frac{\sqrt{6}}{2} - \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\right)\cdot \frac{\sqrt{6}}{2}\\
&=\boxed{\frac{3+2\sqrt{3}}{16}}.
\end{align*}

We can write $\tan x = \sin x$ as $\frac{\sin x}{\cos x} = \sin x,$ so
\[\sin x = \cos x \sin x.\]Then $\sin x - \cos x \sin x = 0,$ or $\sin x (1 - \cos x) = 0.$  Thus, $\sin x = 0$ or $\cos x = 1.$

The solutions to $\sin x = 0$ are $x = 0,$ $\pi,$ and $2 \pi.$

The solution to $\cos x = 1$ is $x = 0.$

Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{0, \pi, 2 \pi}.$

We can write
\[\tan x - \sin x = 0.\]Then
\[\frac{\sin x}{\cos x} - \sin x = 0,\]so
\[\sin x (\sec x - 1) = 0.\]Hence, $\sin x = 0$ or $\sec x = 1.$

If $\sin x = 0,$ then $x = 0,$ $x = \pi,$ or any multiple of $\pi.$

Otherwise, $\sec x = 1$ means $\cos x = 1,$ so $x = 2n \pi$ for some integer $n.$

Therefore, the solutions are $\boxed{0,\pi,2n\pi}$ for integers $n.$

If $z^{13} = 1,$ then $z^{13} - 1 = 0,$ which factors as
\[(z - 1)(z^{12} + z^{11} + \dots + z + 1) = 0.\]If $z = 1,$ then $z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} = 6.$

\begin{align*}
a &= z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}, \\
b &= z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}.
\end{align*}Then
\[a + b = (z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}) + (z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}) = -1.\]Also,
\begin{align*}
ab &= (z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12})(z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}) \\
&= z^3 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{12} \\
&\quad + z^5 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{14} \\

&\quad + z^6 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{12} + z^2 \\

&= 3z + 3z^2 + 3z^3 + 3z^4 + 3z^5 + 3z^6 + 3z^7 + 3z^8 + 3z^9 + 3z^{10} + 3z^{11} + 3z^{12} \\
&= -3.

Note that
\begin{align*}
z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} &= z + z^3 + z^4 -z^3 - z^5 -z^6 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} - z^{10} -z^{12} -z^{13} \\
&= \frac{z^{13} - z}{z - 1} + \frac{-z^6 + z^7 + z^8 - z^9}{z^2 - z} + \frac{-z^{10} - z^{11}}{1 - z}.
\end{align*}We see that $z = 1$ is not allowed, since that would cause division by zero.

Since $z^{13} = 1,$ the possible values of $z$ are $e^{2 \pi i j/13}$ for $j = 1,$ 2, $\dots,$ 12.  We can check that $-6,$ $-5,$ $-4,$ $-3,$ $-2,$ $-1,$ and 1 are all roots of
\[\frac{z^{13} - z}{z - 1} = 0.\]The roots of $-z^6 + z^7 + z^8 - z^9 = 0$ can be found as follows:
\begin{align*}
-z^6 + z^7 + z^8 - z^9 &= z^9 \cdot \frac{1}{z^3} - z^9 \cdot \frac{1}{z^2} + z^9 \cdot \frac{1}{z} - z^9 \\
&= \frac{z^{10} - z^9 + z^8 - z^7}{z^2 - z},
\end{align*}so the roots of $-z^6 + z^7 + z^8 - z^9 = 0$ are $e^{2 \pi i j/10}$ for $j = 1,$ 2, $\dots,$ 10.  The roots of $-z^{10} - z^{11} = 0$ are $e^{2 \pi i j/21}$ for $j = 1,$ 2, $\dots,$ 21.

Therefore, the possible values of $z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}$ are
\[e^{2 \pi i j/13} + e^{2 \pi i k/10} + e^{2 \pi i m/21}\]for $1 \le j \le 12,$ $1 \le k \le 10,$ and $1 \le m \le 21.$  By the convolution product,
\begin{align*}
w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \dots + w_k^2 &= \left| \sum_{j = 1}^{12} e^{2 \pi i j/13} \right|^2 + \left| \sum_{j = 1}^{10} e^{2 \pi i j/10} \right|^2 + \left| \sum_{j = 1}^{21} e^{2 \pi i j/21} \right|^2.
\end{align*}Note that $\left| \sum_{j = 1}^{12} e^{2 \pi i j/13} \right| = \left| \sum_{j = 1}^{12} e^{-2 \pi i j/13} \right|,$ so
\begin{align*}
\left| \sum_{j = 1}^{12} e^{2 \pi i j/13} \right|^2 &= \left| \sum_{j = 1}^{12} \left( e^{2 \pi i/13} \right)^{j - 1} \right|^2 \\
&= \left| \frac{1 - \left( e^{2 \pi i/13} \right)^{12}}{1 - e^{2 \pi i/13}} \right|^2 \\
&= \frac{13}{6}.
\end{align*}We compute that
\[\sum_{j = 1}^{10} e^{2 \pi i j/10} = 0,\]so $\left| \sum_{j = 1}^{10} e^{2 \pi i j/10} \right| = 0.$

We also compute that $\sum_{j = 1}^{21} e^{2 \pi i j/21} = 0,$ so $\left| \sum_{j = 1}^{21} e^{2 \pi i j/21} \right| = 0.$

Hence,
\[w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \dots + w_k^2 = \boxed{\frac{13}{6}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
By the scalar triple product, for any vectors $\mathbf{p},$ $\mathbf{q}$ and $\mathbf{r},$
\[\mathbf{p} \cdot (\mathbf{q} \times \mathbf{r}) = \mathbf{q} \cdot (\mathbf{r} \times \mathbf{p}) = \mathbf{r} \cdot (\mathbf{p} \times \mathbf{q}).\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\|^2 \\
&= \boxed{4}.
\end{align*}

Extracting Answer from: 
We have that
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) &= \mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{a}) \\
&= \mathbf{b} (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{a}) - \mathbf{a} (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \\
&= \mathbf{a} (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b}) - \mathbf{b} (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}).
\end{align*}Since $\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\| = 2,$
\[\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\|^2 = 4 = \|a\|^2 \|b\|^2 (\sin^2 \theta),\]where $\theta$ is the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}.$  Thus,
\[\sin^2 \theta = \frac{4}{\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\|(\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}))\|^2 &= \|\mathbf{a} (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b}) - \mathbf{b} (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})\|^2 \\
&= (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2 + (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a}) - 2 (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2 \\
&= (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a}) - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2 \\
&= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 (1 - \cos^2 \theta) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= 4.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\[\|\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})\| = 2.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) \\
&= (\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) \cdot \mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{b} \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) \cdot \mathbf{a} \\
&= (\mathbf{b} \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{a})) \cdot \mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{b})) \cdot \mathbf{a} \\
&= 0.
\end{align*}Hence, $\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) = \boxed{0}.$

We start by factoring the first equation. Since the LHS looks like a difference of squares, we substitute $a=x^2+y^2$, $b=\sqrt{2}x\sqrt{2}y$, so that $a^2-b^2=(x^2-y^2)^2$. If $y=0$ then $x=0$, but this does not satisfy the first equation. Thus, $x^2-3y^2-x=0$. Solving the system of equations yields $x=\sqrt{2}\cos30$, $y=\sqrt{\frac{1}{6}}$. Thus, $x = \boxed{\sqrt{6}\cos{15^{\circ}}}$.

Let the triangle be $ABC,$ where $AB = AC.$  Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$  Let $H$ and $I$ denote the orthocenter and incenter, as usual.  Without loss of generality, we can assume that the inradius of triangle $ABC$ is 1.  As usual, let $a = BC,$ $b = AC,$ and $c = AB.$

[asy]
unitsize(8 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H, I;
real angleA = aCos(1/9);

B = (0,0);
C = (1,0);
A = extension(B, B + dir(90 - angleA/2), C, C + dir(90 + angleA/2));
D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2;
E = (B + reflect(A,C)*(B))/2;
F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2;
H = extension(B,E,C,F);
I = incenter(A,B,C);

draw(A--D,red);
draw(B--E,red);
draw(C--F,red);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(incircle(A,B,C));
draw(B--I);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill);
dot("$I$", I, dir(0));

label("$1$", (H + I)/2, E);
label("$1$", (D + I)/2, E);
label("$\frac{a}{2}$", (B + D)/2, S);
[/asy]

Since $\angle B = \angle C,$ $B = 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2}.$  Then from triangle $BDI,$
\[\tan \angle IBD = \frac{1}{a/2} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Since $\angle IBD = \frac{B}{2} = 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4},$
\[\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a}.\]From triangle $BDH,$
\[\tan \angle HBD = \frac{2}{a/2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From right triangle $ABE,$ $\angle ABE = 90^\circ - A.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\angle HBD &= \angle ABD - \angle ABE \\
&= B - (90^\circ - A) \\
&= A + B - 90^\circ \\
&= A + 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2} - 90^\circ \\
&= \frac{A}{2}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From the equation $\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a},$
\[\frac{\tan 45^\circ - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan 45^\circ \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a},\]or
\[\frac{1 - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Solving, we find
\[\tan \frac{A}{4} = \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}.\]Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \tan \frac{2A}{4} = \frac{2 \cdot \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}}{1 - (\frac{a - 2}{a + 2})^2} = \frac{a^2 - 4}{4a}.\]But $\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a},$ so
\[\frac{a^2 - 4}{4a} = \frac{4}{a}.\]Then $a^2 - 4 = 16,$ so $a^2 = 20.$  It follows that $a = \sqrt{20} = 2 \sqrt{5}.$

Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{16}{8 \sqrt{5}} = \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}.\]Also, $BD = \frac{a}{2} = \sqrt{5},$ so from right triangle $ABD,$
\[AD = \frac{AB}{\tan \frac{A}{2}} = \frac{\sqrt{5}}{2/\sqrt{5}} = \frac{5}{2}.\]By Pythagoras on right triangle $ABD,$
\[AB = \sqrt{5 + \frac{25}{4}} = \frac{3 \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Finally, by the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$

$\cos \angle A$ equals the ratio of the length of the altitude from $A$ to the length of the side opposite $A$.

Let $I$ be the incenter, and let the incircle touch $BC$, $CA$, and $AB$ at $D$, $E$, and $F$, respectively.  Let the altitude from $A$ meet $BC$ at $X$, and let $M$ be the midpoint of $BC$.  Note that $AX=2DM$ and $MX=DB=MC$.  Since $\triangle IEF\cong\triangle IBD$, we have $IE=IB=DB=MC$.  Let $H$ be the foot of the altitude from $I$ to $BC$.  Note that $HX=\frac{1}{2}BC-DM=\frac{1}{2}BC-\frac{1}{4}BC=\frac{1}{4}BC=IE$, so $HX=IE$.  It follows that $\triangle IEH$ and $\triangle MXA$ are congruent, so $\angle \frac{A}{2}=\angle IHX$.  Finally, using Right Triangles $AIH$ and $AIX$, $\cos\frac{A}{2}=\cos\angle IHX=\frac{IH}{IX}=\frac{r}{r_a}$, where $r$ is the inradius and $r_a$ is the exradius of $\triangle ABC$ opposite $A$.  (To see that $IH=r$, note that $\triangle IDH\cong\triangle IEX$, which implies $IH=EX=r$.) Thus, $\cos A = 2\cos^2\frac{A}{2}-1=\boxed{\frac{r^2}{(s-a)(s+b)}}$

By the Triangle Inequality,
\[\|\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k\| \le  \|\mathbf{v}_1\| + \|\mathbf{v}_2\| + \dots + \|\mathbf{v}_k\| = k.\]Then
\[k \ge \left\|\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{61} > \sqrt{49} = 7,\]so $k \ge 8.$

We can express $\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$ as the sum of 8 unit vectors, as shown below, so the smallest possible value of $k$ is $\boxed{8}.$

[asy]

unitsize(1 cm);

int i;
pair A, B, C;

A = 6*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5));
C = (6,-5);
B = intersectionpoint(arc(A,1,-45,0),arc(C,1,90,180));

for (i = 0; i <= 5; ++i) {
  draw(i*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5))--(i + 1)*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5)),red,Arrow(6));
}
draw(A--B,red,Arrow(6));
draw(B--C,red,Arrow(6));

draw((-1,0)--(7,0));
draw((0,-6)--(0,1));

dot("$\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$", (6,-5), SE);
[/asy]

From $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$
\[\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 &= \|\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= 1 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} = \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2}. \quad (*)\]Also from $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$ we can take the dot product with $\mathbf{v},$ to get
\[\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{v} + (\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 1.\]By the scalar triple product, $(\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w},$ so
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = 1 - \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}.\]From equation $(*),$
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= 1 - \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2.
\end{align*}Let $\theta$ be the angle between $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{w}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{u}\|^2 \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \cos^2 \theta \\
&\le \frac{1}{2}.
\end{align*}Equality occurs when $\mathbf{u} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ and $\mathbf{w} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1/2 \\ 1/2 \end{pmatrix},$ so the largest possible value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Let $\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}.$  Then
\[\|\mathbf{x}\| = \|\mathbf{u}\| \|\mathbf{v}\| \sin \theta = \sin \theta,\]where $\theta$ is the angle between $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{v}.$  Since $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{v}$ are unit vectors, $\|\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\| = \|\mathbf{v}\| = 1.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\| &= \|\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} - \mathbf{w}\| \\
&\le \|\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\| + \|\mathbf{w}\| \\
&= 2.
\end{align*}Then $|\mathbf{x} \cdot \mathbf{w}| \le 2 \|\mathbf{x}\| = 2 \sin \theta,$ so $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ attains a largest possible value of $\boxed{2}$ (for example, when $\mathbf{w} = \mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}$).

From the product-to-sum formula, $\cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ = \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ),$ so
\begin{align*}
\cos 10^\circ \cos 30^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ &= \cos 10^\circ \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cos 10^\circ (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} (\cos 10^\circ \cos 120^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right).
\end{align*}Applying the product-to-sum formula again, we get
\begin{align*}
\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right) &= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \frac{\cos 30^\circ + \cos 10^\circ}{2} \right) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{8} \cos 30^\circ \\
&= \boxed{\frac{3}{16}}.
\end{align*}

Let $z = \operatorname{cis} 10^\circ.$  Then $z$ satisfies $z^{36} = 1.$  (Note that $36$ is a multiple of $4,$ which is the period of the cosine function.)

We can label the roots of $z^{36} - 1 = 0$ as $1,$ $z_1,$ $z_2,$ $\dots,$ $z_{35}.$  Note that
\begin{align*}
\cos 10^\circ \cos 30^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ &= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \cos 30^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{8} \cdot (z_1 + 1) (z_2 + 1) (z_3 + 1),
\end{align*}where the factor of 1/2 comes from $\cos 10^\circ = \frac{1}{2} (z + \overline{z}).$

The roots other than 1 alternate in pairs in the form $z_k$ and $z_{36 - k}.$  Therefore,
\begin{align*}
(z_1 + 1) (z_2 + 1) (z_3 + 1) &= [(z_1 + 1)(z_{35} + 1)][(z_2 + 1)(z_{34} + 1)][(z_3 + 1)(z_{33} + 1)] \\
&= [(z_1 z_{35} + z_1 + z_{35} + 1)][(z_2 z_{34} + z_2 + z_{34} + 1)][(z_3 z_{33} + z_3 + z_{33} + 1)] \\
&= [(z_1 z_{35} + z_{35} + 1 - z_1 \cdot 1)][(z_2 z_{34} + z_{34} + 1 - z_2 \cdot 1)][(z_3 z_{33} + z_{33} + 1 - z_3 \cdot 1)] \\
&= [(z_1 z_{35} + z_{35} - z_1)][(z_2 z_{34} + z_{34} - z_2)][(z_3 z_{33} + z_{33} - z_3)] \\
&= [(z_1 - 1)(z_{35} - 1)][(z_2 - 1)(z_{34} - 1)][(z_3 - 1)(z_{33} - 1)] \\
&= (z - 1)^3 z^{33} \\
&= z^{36} - 3z^{35} + 3z^{34} - z^3 \\
&= -z^3 - 3z^{35} + 3z^{34} + 1 \\
&= -z^{33} - 3z^{23} + 3z^{13} + 1 \\
&= -(z^{36} + z^{24} - z^{12} - 1) - 3(z^{36} + z^{12}) + 3(z^{36} + z^{22}) + 1 \\
&= -1 - 3z^{12} + 3z^{22} + 3z^{24} - 2z^{36} \\
&= -1 - 3 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} + 3 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot \frac{1}{2} + 3 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} - 2 \\
&= \boxed{\frac{3 \sqrt{3}}{16}}.
\end{align*}

A $60^\circ$ rotation around the origin in the counter-clockwise direction corresponds to multiplication by $\operatorname{cis} 60^\circ = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i.$

[asy]
unitsize(0.5 cm);

pair A = (3*sqrt(3),-5), B = rotate(60)*(A);

draw((0,-6)--(0,3));

draw((0,0)--B,dashed);

dot("$3 \sqrt{3} - 5i$", A, S);

[/asy]

\[(3 \sqrt{3} - 5i) \left( \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i \right) = \boxed{4 \sqrt{3} + 2i}.\]

Note the similarity of the recursion to the angle addition identity
\[\tan (x + y) = \frac{\tan x + \tan y}{1 - \tan x \tan y}.\]We can take advantage of this similarity as follows: Let $f_1 = 3,$ $f_2 = 2,$ and let $f_n = f_{n - 1} + f_{n - 2}$ for all $n \ge 3.$  Let $\theta_n = \frac{f_n \pi}{12}.$  Then $\tan \theta_1 = \tan \frac{\pi}{4} = 1$ and $\tan \theta_2 = \tan \frac{\pi}{6} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}.$  Also,
\begin{align*}
\tan \theta_{n + 2} &= \tan (\theta_{n + 1} + \theta_n) \\
&= \frac{\tan \theta_{n + 1} + \tan \theta_n}{1 - \tan \theta_n \tan \theta_{n + 1}}.
\end{align*}Since the sequences $(a_n)$ and $(\tan \theta_n)$ have the same initial terms, and the same recursion, they coincide.

Since $\tan \theta$ is periodic with period $\pi,$ to compute further terms of $\tan \theta_n,$ it suffices to compute $f_n$ modulo 12:
\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
n & f_n \pmod{12} \\ \hline
1 & 3 \\
2 & 2 \\
3 & 5 \\
4 & 7 \\
5 & 0 \\
6 & 7 \\
7 & 7 \\
8 & 2 \\
9 & 9 \\
10 & 11 \\
11 & 8 \\
12 & 7 \\
13 & 3 \\
14 & 10 \\
15 & 1 \\
16 & 11 \\
17 & 0 \\
18 & 11 \\
19 & 11 \\
20 & 10 \\
21 & 9 \\
22 & 7 \\
23 & 4 \\
24 & 11 \\
25 & 3 \\
26 & 2
\end{array}
\]Since $a_{25} \equiv a_1 \pmod{12}$ and $a_{26} \equiv a_2 \pmod{12},$ the sequence modulo 12 become periodic at this point, with period 12.

Therefore,
\[a_{2009} = \tan \theta_{2009} = \tan \theta_5 = \boxed{0}.\]

[asy] unitsize(3cm);   pair A,B,C,D,EE,F; A=(0,0); B=(1,0); EE=(2.5,-0.5); C=(3,0); D=(3.5,-0.5); F=(4,0);  draw(A--B--EE--C--D--F, linetype("4 4")); draw(Circle((1.5,-0.5), 0.5));  label("$\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}$", A, SW); label("$1$", B, S); label("$a_3$", C, S); label("$a_4$", D, S); label("$a_5$", F, S);  pair[] ps={A,B,C,D,F,EE}; dot(ps); [/asy]
The first few terms suggest that most terms will be positive, so let $a_n = \tan b_n$, which gives us $0 < b_n < \frac {\pi}{2}$. We then have \begin{align*} \tan b_{n + 2} & = \frac {\tan b_{n} + \tan b_{n + 1}}{1 - \tan b_{n}\tan b_{n + 1}} \\ & = \frac {\sin (b_n + b_{n + 1})}{\cos b_n\cos b_{n + 1} - \sin b_n\sin b_{n + 1}} \\ & = \tan (b_n + b_{n + 1}).  \end{align*}Therefore, $b_n = 2^{n - 2}b_2 + 2^{n - 3}b_1$, so $a_n = \tan b_n = \tan \left( {2^{n - 2}b_2 + 2^{n - 3}b_1} \right) = \tan \left( 2^{n - 3}b_1 \right)$.

Therefore, $a_{2009} = \tan \left( 2^{2006} b_1 \right)$. We can find an expression for $\tan b_1$ as follows: \begin{align*} a_3 & = \tan b_3 \\ & = \tan \left( b_1 + b_2 \right) \\ & = \frac {\tan b_1 + \tan b_2}{1 - \tan b_1\tan b_2} \\ & = \frac {\tan b_1 + \frac {1}{\sqrt {3}}}{1 - \frac {1}{\sqrt3} \tan b_1}. \end{align*}Solving for $\tan b_1$, we find $\tan b_1 = \frac {3 - 2\sqrt {3}}{3}$. \[\mathbf{a} = t \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} t \\ 2t \end{pmatrix}\]for some scalar $t.$  Then
\[\mathbf{b} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} t \\ 2t \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 - t \\ 7 - 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]We want this to be orthogonal to $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix},$ so

Extracting Answer from: 
In general, By DeMoivre's Theorem,
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{cis} n \theta &= (\operatorname{cis} \theta)^n \\
&= (\cos \theta + i \sin \theta)^n \\
&= \cos^n \theta + \binom{n}{1} i \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta - \binom{n}{3} i \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dotsb.
\end{align*}Matching real and imaginary parts, we get
\begin{align*}
\cos n \theta &= \cos^n \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \binom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb, \\
\sin n \theta &= \binom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \binom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
\tan n \theta &= \frac{\sin n \theta}{\cos n \theta} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb}{\cos^n \theta - \dbinom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \tan \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \tan^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \tan^5 \theta - \dotsb}{1 - \dbinom{n}{2} \tan^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \tan^4 \theta - \dotsb}.
\end{align*}Taking $n = 9,$ we get
\[\tan 9 \theta = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Note that for $\theta = 5^\circ,$ $25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $165^\circ,$ $\tan 9 \theta = \tan 45^\circ = 1.$  Thus,
\[1 = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Let $t = \tan \theta,$ so
\[1 = \frac{9t - 84t^3 + 126t^5 - 36t^7 + t^9}{1 - 36t^2 + 126t^4 - 84t^6 + 9t^8}.\]Thus, $\tan 5^\circ,$ $\tan 25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $\tan 165^\circ$ are the roots of
\[t^9 - 9t^8 - 36t^7 + 84t^6 + 126t^5 - 126t^4 - 84t^3 + 36t^2 + 9t - 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, their sum is $\boxed{9}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Multiplying both sides by $zw(z + w),$ we get
\[zw = (z + w)^2,\]which simplifies to $w^2 + zw + z^2 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[w = \frac{-1 \pm i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot z,\]so the solutions are $w = z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$ and $w = z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ,$ which means that $P$ is an equilateral triangle.

[asy]
usepackage("amsmath");

unitsize(2 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = dir(20);
B = dir(20 + 120);
C = dir(20 + 240);

draw(Circle((0,0),1));
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0));
draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2));

dot("$z$", A, A);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$", B, B);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ$", C, SW);
[/asy]

The side length of the equilateral triangle is
\[\left| z - \frac{-1 + i \sqrt{3}}{2} z \right| = \left| \frac{3 - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \right| |z| = \sqrt{3} \cdot 2,\]so the area of the equilateral triangle is
\[\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cdot (2 \sqrt{3})^2 = \boxed{3 \sqrt{3}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $z = 2\operatorname{cis} \theta$ where $0 < \theta < \frac{\pi}{2}$. Then
\[\frac{1}{z + w} - \frac{1}{z}-\frac{1}{w}  = 0\]Rearranging, we have
\begin{align*}
\frac1w &= \frac{1}{z+w} - \frac 1z = \frac z{z(z+w)} - \frac{z+w}{z(z+w)} = \frac{-w}{z(z+w)} \\
\implies w &= \frac{-z^2}{z+zw} = -z\cdot \frac{z}{z+w} = -2\operatorname{cis}\theta \cdot \frac{2\operatorname{cis}\theta}{2\operatorname{cis}\theta + w}.
\end{align*}Thus, letting $w = 2\operatorname{cis} \phi$ for some angle $\phi \in (0, 2\pi)$, we have
\[2\operatorname{cis}\phi = -4e^{i\theta} \cdot \frac{2e^{i\theta}}{2e^{i\theta} + 2e^{i\phi}} = -4 \cdot \frac{e^{2i\theta}}{e^{i(\theta+\phi)}+e^{i\theta}}.\]Letting $u = \frac{\theta + \phi}{2}$ and $v = \frac{\phi - \theta}{2}$, we have 
\[2\cos\phi + 4i\sin\phi = -4e^{2i\theta} \cdot \frac{e^{i(\theta + \phi)}}{2\cos u\cdot e^{iv} + 2i\sin u}.\]Then equating real parts gives 
\[2\cos\phi = \frac{-4\cos(2\theta)\cos(\theta+\phi)}{\cos u\left(\cos u \cos v - \sin u \sin v\right)} = \frac{-2\cos\theta\cos(\theta+\phi)}{\cos u \cos v - \sin u \sin v},\]which simplifies to  \[2\cos u\cos v - 2\cos\theta \cos(\theta+\phi)\sin u \sin v = -\cos \phi.\]Since $\phi$ is a variable constant and $\theta$ is fixed, this describes a family of hyperbolas, with the asymptotes $v = \pm \theta$ and $uv$- intercepts $\left(\frac{\pi}{4},\pm \frac{\pi}{4}\right).$  The case $\phi = \theta$ corresponds to the locus of $w$ such that the triangle $0,z,w$ is equilateral, so the intersection point of the hyperbolas corresponds to $w = -4\operatorname{cis}\theta,$ which is not a vertex of the polygon $P.$  Furthermore, we can solve for $w$ given $\phi$ as in the above calculations, hence the polygon $P$ is the hexagon formed from the vertices $z, -4\operatorname{cis}\theta$ and the intersection points of the hyperbolas (one double point and four distinct points).  By the Shoelace Formula, the area is
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{2} \left|\operatorname{Im}\left(-4e^{i\theta} \cdot \frac{2e^{i\theta}}{2e^{i\theta} + (2\cos\theta,\pm 2\sin\theta)}\right) + \left(-4e^{i(\pi-\theta)} \cdot \frac{2e^{i(\pi -\theta)}}{2e^{i(\pi-\theta)} + (2\cos\theta,\pm 2\sin\theta)}\right) + (0,0)-2e^{i\theta}(0,0)\right| \\
+ \frac{1}{2}\left|\operatorname{Im}\left(-4e^{i\theta} \cdot \frac{-2e^{i(\frac{\theta}{2}+\frac{\pi}{4})}}{2\cos\left(\frac{\theta+\phi}{2}\right)e^{i\frac{\phi-\theta}{2}} + 2i\sin\left(\frac{\theta+\phi}{2}\right)}\right) + \cdots + \left(-4e^{i\theta} \cdot \frac{-2e^{i(\frac{\theta}{2}-\frac{\pi}{4})}}{2\cos\left(\frac{\theta+\phi}{2}\right)e^{-i\frac{\phi-\theta}{2}} - 2i\sin\left(\frac{\theta+\phi}{2}\right)}\right)\right|
\end{align*}where $\pm$ is chosen independently for each of the four intersection points of the hyperbolas. Since the terms involved in the second summation are pairs that differ only by conjugation, the imaginary parts cancel pairwise, and the area of the hexagon reduces to
\[A = \frac{3\sqrt{3}}{2} - 2\sqrt{3} - 2\pi. \square\]

In cylindrical coordinates, $z$ simply denotes the $z$-coordinate of a point.  Thus, for a fixed $z$-coordinate $c,$ all the points lie on a plane that is parallel to the $xy$-plane.  The answer is $\boxed{\text{(C)}}.$

currentlight = (1,0,1);

draw((0,0,-2)--(0,0,0.2));
draw((0,0,0.5)--(0,0,2));

The normal vector to the plane will be orthogonal to both
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}\]and
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix}.\]Their cross product is
\[\begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} (b - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(b - 1) \end{pmatrix}.\]By scaling, we can take $\begin{pmatrix} 1/(1 - a) \\ 1/(1 - b) \\ 1/(1 - c) \end{pmatrix}$ as the normal vector.  Since the plane passes through $(0,0,0),$ the equation of the plane is
\[\frac{x}{1 - a} + \frac{y}{1 - b} + \frac{z}{1 - c} = 0.\]Since the plane passes through $(a,1,1),$
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 0.\]Adding 1 to both sides, we get
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + 1 + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 1,\]so
\[\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = \boxed{1}.\]

\[m \cdot (a,1,1) = m \cdot (1,b,1) = m \cdot (1,1,c) = m \cdot (0,0,0),\]which expands as

Subtracting the second column from the first column, we get
\[
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x - 7 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 - 343 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = 7,$ so this is one possible value of $x.$

Subtracting the third column from the first column, we get
\[
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = -2,$ so this is another possible value of $x.$

Expanding the last determinant along the first column, we get
\begin{align*}
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} &= -(x + 2) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} + (x^3 + 8) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 7 & -2 \end{vmatrix} \\
&= -9x^3 + 351x + 630 = 0.
\end{align*}Since we know that $x = 7$ are $x = -2$ are two solutions, we can take out a factor of $x - 7$ and $x + 2$, to get
\[-9(x - 7)(x + 2)(x + 5) = 0.\]Hence, the possible values of $x$ are $\boxed{7, -2, \text{ and } -5}.$

(Alternatively, by Vieta's formulas, the sum of the three roots of $-9x^3 + 351x + 630 = 0$ is 0, so the third root is $-7 - (-2) = -5.$)

We have that
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|}.\]Let $d = \|\mathbf{a}\| = \|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\|.$  Since $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ $\mathbf{c}$ are mutually orthogonal, $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} = 0.$  Hence,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = d^2.\]Also,
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 &= (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \\
&= \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c} \cdot \mathbf{c} + 2(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \\
&= d^2 + d^2 + d^2 \\
&= 3d^2.
\end{align*}Hence, $\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| = d \sqrt{3},$ so
\[\cos \theta = \frac{d^2}{d \cdot d \sqrt{3}} = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}.\]

GPT File: datasets/persona_short_answer/persona_short_answer/precalculus/904_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Note that
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 &= (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + 2 \mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) + \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 + 2 \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} + \|\mathbf{c}\|^2.
\end{align*}Since any pair of $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ $\mathbf{c}$ are orthogonal,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) = 0,\]and $\|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\|.$  Hence,
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 &= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{c}\|^2 \\
&= 3 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2.
\end{align*}Thus,
\begin{align*}
\cos \theta &= \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \cdot \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|} \\
&= \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \cdot \sqrt{3} \|\mathbf{a}\|} \\
&= \frac{\|\mathbf{b}\| \cdot \|\mathbf{c}\|}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \cdot \sqrt{3} \|\mathbf{a}\|} \\
&= \frac{\cos 90^\circ}{\sqrt{3} \cos 0^\circ} \\
&= \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}.

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\angle A,$ $\angle B,$ $\angle C$ form an arithmetic sequence, $2 \angle B = \angle A + \angle C.$  Then
\[3 \angle B = \angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180^\circ,\]which means $\angle B = 60^\circ,$ and $\angle A + \angle C = 120^\circ.$

Let $h$ be the altitude from $B.$

[asy]
unitsize (1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D;

A = (0,0);
B = 5*dir(40);
C = (5,0);
D = (B.x,0);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D);

label("$A$", A, SW);
label("$B$", B, N);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$h$", (B + D)/2, E);
[/asy]

Then
\[h = AB - BC = \frac{h}{\sin A} - \frac{h}{\sin C},\]so $1 = \frac{1}{\sin A} - \frac{1}{\sin C}.$  Hence,
\[\sin C - \sin A = \sin A \sin C.\]We can write this as
\[2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} \cos \frac{A + C}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) - \cos (A + C)}{2}.\]Since $A + C = 120^\circ,$
\[\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) + \frac{1}{2}}{2} = \frac{\cos (C - A) + \frac{1}{2}}{2}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} &= 2 \cos (C - A) + 1 \\
&= 2 \left( 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} \right) + 1 \\
&= 3 - 4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2},
\end{align*}so
\[4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} + 4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 3 = 0.\]This factors as
\[\left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 1 \right) \left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} + 3 \right) = 0.\]Thus, the only possible value of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Let $x$ be the common difference in the arithmetic sequence.  Then $\angle A = B - x$ and $\angle C = B + x.$

[asy]
unitsize(0.8 cm);

pair A, B, C, D;

B = (0,0);
C = (1,0);
A = intersectionpoint(arc(B,sqrt(3),0,180),arc(C,2,0,180));
D = foot(B,A,C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
[/asy]

Then we can set up a right triangle as shown, where $BD = AB - BC.$  Then from the triangle, $\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \frac{BD}{AB + BC}.$

Let $y = AB$ and $z = BC.$  Then $AC = y + z,$ so by the Pythagorean Theorem,
\[y^2 + z^2 = (y + z)^2 - (y + z) BD.\]Then $y^2 + z^2 - yz = yz - BD \cdot yz,$ or
\[BD \cdot yz = y^2 + z^2 - 2yz.\]By similar triangles,
\[\frac{BD}{z} = \frac{x}{y - x},\]so $BD = \frac{xz}{y - x}.$  Substituting into the equation above, we get
\[\frac{xz^2}{y - x} = y^2 + z^2 - 2yz.\]This simplifies to $xz + y^2 = 2yz.$

Also, $\angle B = 180^\circ - \angle A - \angle C = 180^\circ - 2B,$ so $B = 60^\circ.$  Then $x = 60^\circ,$ so $2yz = xz + y^2 = 2yz,$ which means both $x = y$ and $y = z.$  Then $\angle A = 0^\circ,$ $\angle B = 60^\circ,$ and $\angle C = 120^\circ.$

Then $\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \sin 60^\circ = \boxed{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}}.$

By the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality,
\[(\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w})^2 \le \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 = 3^2 \cdot 4^2 = 144.\]Hence, $|\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}| \le 12.$

Note that if $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 0 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix}$ and $\mathbf{w} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 3 \\ 4 \end{pmatrix},$ then $\|\mathbf{v}\| = 3$ and $\|\mathbf{w}\| = 5,$ and
\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} = 0 \cdot 2 + 3 \cdot 0 + 4 \cdot (-1) = -4.\]Therefore, the minimum value is $\boxed{-12}.$

\[\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5} \begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{5}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{5}{14} \end{pmatrix} \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then $\frac{13}{14} a - \frac{1}{7} b + \frac{3}{14} = 0,$ $-\frac{1}{7} a + \frac{5}{7} b + \frac{3}{7} c = 0,$ and $\frac{3}{14} a + \frac{3}{7} b + \frac{5}{14} = 0.$  These reduce to
\begin{align*}
13a - 2b + 3c &= 0, \\
-a + 5b + 3c &= 0, \\
3a + 6b + 5c &= 0.
\end{align*}Subtracting the first two equations, we get $14a - 7b = 0,$ so $b = 2a.$  Then
\[-a + 10a + 3c = 0,\]so $c = -3a.$  Hence,
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a \\ 2a \\ -3a \end{pmatrix} = a \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix},\]so the vector we seek is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}}.$
